# Chapter 1

## CHAPTER 1

Freedom was a quota now. My daily allowance: 1.2 cubic meters of personal space, 17 minutes of unmonitored thought, and the right to complain about the weather, provided you didn't mention why it was happening. I'd already spent the space, the thoughts were running low, and the sky was the color of a forgotten bruise.



Arthur lay on his back, the single top sheet tangled around his ankles, and watched the snail. He’d been watching it for seventeen minutes. The clock on his nightstand, a plastic solar-powered thing shaped like a cheerful sun, had given up two days prior. Its face was dark. But Arthur knew it was seventeen minutes because he’d started counting when the first bead of sweat, distinct from the general dampness, had traced a path from his temple into his ear. That was a countable event.

The air in the room was a solid thing. It had texture—a woolen, suffocating consistency that smelled of stale laundry and the ghost of last week’s lentil soup. The ceiling fan hung motionless, a dead metal spider. The Great Brownout had hit the grid three days ago. It wasn’t a dramatic, spark-flying collapse. It was a sigh. A national exhalation of defeat. The power had simply… lessened, then faded, like a battery-operated toy winding down. Now, the hum of civilization was replaced by the thrum of heat and the distant, frantic percussion of someone, somewhere, trying to start a generator.

Arthur’s phone was a brick. His laptop was a very expensive paperweight. His smart fridge was just a box, growing warmer, its contents embarking on their slow, odorous journey back to constituent elements. He’d read the pamphlets, of course. *Preparedness in the Anthropocene*. He had the five-gallon water jugs (two of them full), the bucket of rice (weevily), the solar-powered radio (which got one station playing uninterrupted smooth jazz, as if the apocalypse was a hotel lobby). He was prepared for an emergency. He was not prepared for the profound, boring *stillness* of it.

The snail moved. A millimetre. A conquest.

“What’s your plan?” Arthur asked it, his voice raspy from disuse. “You going to cross the glass? Reach the frame? Then what? The great beyond is my IKEA dresser. It’s particleboard. It won’t sustain you.”

The snail did not answer. It was a creature of pure, damp intention.

With a groan that was mostly mental but partially vocal, Arthur peeled himself from the mattress. The sensation was like separating two layers of clingfilm. He stood, the wooden floorboards warm under his bare feet. He was a man of forty-two, of average height and build, whose most distinctive feature was a look of perpetual, mild bewilderment, as if he’d been told a joke in a foreign language he almost understood. He wore boxer shorts and a t-shirt for a 5K fun run he’d never run, the logo cracked and faded.

He shuffled to the window, eye-level with the mollusc. Up close, it was magnificent in its alien-ness. Its eyestalks were extended, tiny black dots regarding the dim room. Arthur pressed a finger against the glass next to it. “I envy your simplicity. Your needs are moisture and algae. My needs include Wi-Fi and a functioning dehumidifier. I am losing the evolutionary race.”

A thump came from downstairs. Then a curse, vivid and inventive.

Arthur left the snail to its pilgrimage and descended into the soup of the house’s lower atmosphere. It was thicker here, pooled with heat. The living room was a museum of abandoned hobbies: a half-strung ukulele, a basket of felting wool (feltable in a previous climate), a stack of books on urban beekeeping. In the centre of it all, like a monolith, stood the Cooler.

It was not, technically, a cooler. It was a repurposed antique hope chest, lined with foil insulation and filled with the last of the block ice from the camping store raid. Inside, floating in meltwater, were the precious things: butter, cheese, the last six eggs, a bottle of insulin for the neighbour, Mrs. Gunderson. And one can of beer. A ceremonial artifact.

Bent over the chest, her head nearly inside it, was Marnie. She was thirty-eight, dressed in cargo shorts and a tank top, her dark hair piled in a messy knot secured by a chopstick. She was muttering.

“The butter’s going swimmy. The cheese is sweating. It’s like a sauna for dairy in here. The ice is losing its will to live.”

“It’s entropy,” Arthur said, leaning against the doorframe. “The universe’s arrow points toward soup.”

Marnie emerged, her face flushed. “The universe can point its arrow somewhere else. I need it to point at a cold breeze.” She stood, hands on hips, surveying their domain. “We need a system. A rota. You watch the ice for the first half of the day, I’ll watch it for the second. We need to fan it. Create airflow.”

“Fan the ice.”

“Yes, Arthur. Fan the ice. With a magazine. Or a piece of cardboard. We have to work with what we have.”

“What we have is a hope chest full of cold water and a single beer waiting for a special occasion that will never come.”

“The occasion is not losing our minds,” Marnie said, picking up a *National Geographic* from 2019 (The Future of Coral!). She began to flap it vigorously over the open chest. A feeble, warm breeze stirred the air. “See? Action. Movement. We are not snails.”

Arthur thought of the snail, inching across the glass with glacial purpose. “Snails are action. Just… slower action.”

“Snails are going to inherit the earth because they’re the only ones who won’t notice it’s become a steam room.” She stopped fanning, breathless. “We need more ice.”

“The camping store is picked clean. It’s a shrine to poor planning and regret.”

“Then we make it.”

“We… make ice.”

“Yes, Arthur. We make ice. The old way. The way of pioneers and… and people in movies. We need a freezer. A working freezer.”

Arthur gestured vaguely to the kitchen, where their stainless steel freezer sat, dark and silent. “We have a freezer. It’s a cabinet.”

“Not that freezer.” A light came into Marnie’s eyes. It was the light that had preceded the ukulele, the felting, the beekeeping. It was the light of a Project. “The community centre. They had that big industrial freezer in the kitchen. For the seniors’ fish sticks.”

“The power is out everywhere, Marnie.”

“But a big freezer,” she said, pacing now, the magazine held like a sceptre. “It’s insulated. It’s a cavern of cold. The cold will linger. It’s a thermal mass. There could be… residual chill. Enough to freeze water if we put it in there. A last bastion of refrigeration.”

“That’s not how thermodynamics works.”

“How do you know? Have you tried? We’ve been sitting here for three days watching the butter melt. That’s not thermodynamics, that’s tragedy. I’m proposing a quest.”

Arthur looked at her. At the determined set of her jaw, the sweat dampening her tank top. He looked at the hope chest cooler, at the dark ceiling fan, at the world outside the window, yellow and still. A quest. To make ice. It was the most absurd thing he’d ever heard.

“What would we even carry the ice home in?” he asked, a capitulation.

Marnie’s grin was triumphant. She pointed to the corner, behind the felting wool. “The picnic hamper. The one with the leather straps. It’s insulated. It’s dignified.”

And so, twenty minutes later, they were on the street, looking like characters from a particularly desperate English novel. Arthur carried the large, wicker picnic hamper. Marnie carried two empty stainless-steel water bottles to fill for the experiment. They had donned wide-brimmed hats. The street was a surreal tableau.

Old Mr. Henderson was on his porch, methodically painting his white wicker furniture with a pale, reflective silver paint. “Albedo effect,” he called out, not looking up. “Increase reflectivity. Reduce heat absorption. Science.”

A few houses down, the Carver twins, aged about ten, had set up a lemonade stand. The sign read: **COLD WATER - 1 BOTTLECAP**. Their cooler was a red plastic wagon filled with hose water. It was the busiest spot on the block.

The air was a physical presence, a weight that made breathing feel like a conscious effort. The sky was that same sickly yellow, the sun a blurred, malevolent coin behind the haze. The trees were listless, their leaves hanging like limp hands. The only sound was the *thwock-thwock-thwock* of a distant sprinkler, hitting parched grass and hot pavement.

“This is madness,” Arthur said, adjusting the strap of the hamper on his shoulder. “We are going to a public building to loot freezer space.”

“We are not looting. We are utilizing underused civic infrastructure. It’s pragmatic.” Marnie stepped over a crack in the pavement that had widened in the heat. “Look, there’s a precedent. In heatwaves in the old days, people went to libraries. For the cool.”

“Libraries had air conditioning. The community centre has a dead freezer and the lingering smell of boiled cabbage.”

“Pessimism is a luxury we can’t afford,” Marnie said, striding ahead. “We must court the absurd. Embrace the irrational solution. It’s the only domain left to us.”

They turned onto the main commercial street. The shops were mostly closed, their awnings stretched like slack jaws. A few were open, doors propped wide, shopkeepers sitting on stools fanning themselves with newspapers. The smooth jazz from Arthur’s radio seemed to emanate from the walls themselves. A dog lay in a strip of shade, panting so heavily its whole body shook.

Then, they saw the line.

It stretched from the door of the community centre, down the ramp, around the corner of the building. Fifty people, maybe more. They held pots, bowls, Tupperware containers, lunchboxes. They stood in a silence broken only by the shuffling of feet and the occasional cough.

Arthur and Marnie stopped at the back of the line. A woman in front of them, holding a ceramic mixing bowl, turned. She had the serene, exhausted look of a pilgrim.

“What’s the queue for?” Arthur asked, though he dreaded the answer.

“The freezer,” the woman said, her voice flat. “They’re letting people put one item in for two hours. On a rota. Sign-up sheet’s inside. I’m here for my butter.”

Marnie’s face fell, then hardened with resolve. She looked at the dignified picnic hamper, then at the line of people with their humble containers. “Right. Well. We’re here now.”

The line moved with a slowness that made Arthur think of his snail. He watched a man in a suit jacket (why?) carefully place a single Ziploc bag of what looked like stew into the hamper of a volunteer at the door, who noted it on a clipboard. This was the system. This was the new economy: not bottlecaps, but residual chill.

After an hour, they were inside the dim foyer. The air was marginally cooler, a memory of stone and bureaucracy. The sign-up sheet was a thing of beauty and despair. Neat columns: **Name. Item. Time In. Time Out (STRICT)**. The items were a poem of perishability: *Mother’s Insulin, 1 vial. Salmon fillet. Goat cheese. Chocolate bar (dark). Sourdough starter.*

Marnie signed them up for a slot two hours hence. *Arthur & Marnie. 2x Water Bottles (to freeze).*

The volunteer, a tired-looking man with a “Community Champion” lanyard, eyed their hamper. “One item per household slot.”

“They’re a pair,” Marnie said, with authority. “Symbiotic. You can’t freeze one without the other.”

The man sighed, too hot to argue. “Fine. B-17. Bottom shelf. Don’t move anyone else’s stuff.”

They were ushered into the large hall. The seniors’ exercise mats were stacked against a wall. In the centre of the room, like an altar, stood the industrial freezer. Its door was propped open. A crowd gathered around it, not pushing, just… watching. A reverent hush. People took turns approaching, kneeling, sliding their container into the assigned spot. It was a ritual. A sacrament of cold.

Arthur felt a laugh bubble up in his chest, a desperate, airy thing. He swallowed it. This wasn’t funny. This was the most serious thing in the world. The preservation of butter. The salvation of cheese. The quest for ice.

As they waited for their turn, he watched the faces in the dim light. The focused intensity of a young man slotting a small bottle of breast milk next to a Tupperware of potato salad. The quiet despair of an elderly woman clutching a single yogurt cup. This was the pulse of the new world. Not rebellion, not heroics. Logistics. The grim, shared mathematics of decay.

When their time came, Marnie approached with the water bottles. She placed them in slot B-17 with the care of a curator handling priceless artifacts. She closed the giant freezer door, just for a moment, to let the cold—what was left of it—settle. The *thunk* echoed in the silent hall.

They emerged back into the yellow glare two hours later, the picnic hamper noticeably heavier. The water bottles, nestled in a towel, were cold to the touch. Not frozen solid, but slushy. A partial victory. A miracle of residual thermal mass.

The walk home was quieter. The weight in the hamper was a kind of hope, but a fragile, melting one. As they turned onto their street, Arthur saw it. On their front porch, sitting primly on the wicker chair, was a stranger.

He was an older man, impeccably dressed in a linen suit that looked crisp despite the heat. He wore a Panama hat. Beside him was a small, hard-sided leather suitcase. He was not sweating. He appeared to be simply… existing, comfortably, in the soup.

As Arthur and Marnie approached, he stood, tipping his hat.

“Arthur Bell? Marnie Vance?” His voice was mellifluous, cool.

“Who’s asking?” Marnie said, shifting the hamper’s weight.

“My name is Alistair Finch.” He smiled, a perfect, practised thing. “I’m here about your carbon.”

Arthur blinked. “Our… carbon?”

“Your carbon debt. More specifically, your surplus.” Mr. Finch’s eyes twinkled, as if sharing a delightful secret. “It appears, against all odds, you are in credit. And I am here to settle the account.”

He patted the leather suitcase. It made a soft, solid *thump*.

The world was a steam room. The butter was swimmy. The snail was on its journey. And now, a man in a linen suit was on their porch, talking about carbon like it was a bank balance.

Arthur looked at Marnie. Marnie looked at the suitcase. The ice in their hamper gave a faint, sloshing sigh.

The day the air conditioning died was getting interesting.

[Chapter 1 End - 2,498 words]

---

# Chapter 2

## CHAPTER 2

The ice, in its dignified hamper, sat on the floor between them like a third guest. Mr. Alistair Finch sat in Arthur’s favourite armchair, the one with the faint stain from the felting incident, and made it look like a throne. He had declined water—“I’m quite hydrated, thank you”—and had instead produced a silver flask from his inner pocket, taking a small, appreciative sip. It smelled, faintly, of peppermint and something colder.

“Let me be clear from the outset,” Finch said, recapping the flask. “I do not represent a government agency. Not the Carbon Compliance Office, not the Department of Environmental Accountability, not the Ministry of Offsetting. Those are… blunt instruments. Ledger-keepers.” He said the words with gentle distaste.

“Then who do you represent?” Marnie asked. She hadn’t sat down. She stood by the hope chest cooler, arms crossed, a sentinel guarding the swimmy butter.

“I am a private auditor. A curator, if you will. For the Carbon Reparation & Liquidity Foundation. CRLF. We deal in the… finer points of the carbon economy. The human element.” His gaze swept the room, taking in the ukulele, the felting wool, the books. It was not a judgmental look, but an appraising one, like a botanist identifying species. “Your file is fascinating. Truly.”

Arthur found his voice. It came out dry, like the rustle of dead leaves. “We don’t have a file. We have a recycling bin. And we’re very diligent about it.”

Finch’s laugh was a soft, pleasant sound. “Diligence! A virtue. But the bin is not the ledger. The ledger is everything. Every bus not taken, every light bulb unscrewed, every avocado refused because of its food-mile provenance. Every sigh of existential dread directed at the state of the planet—that’s a micro-carbon credit, Arthur. A tiny, negative emission.” He leaned forward, his hands steepled. “You two have been sighing, metaphorically and literally, for years. You live in a 1,200-square-foot detached house, yet you heat only one room in winter. You own a car, a 2014 hybrid, but you use it primarily as a greenhouse for propagating herbs. Your most frequent travel is by bicycle, and even then, you plot routes to maximize downhill coasting. You compost with the zeal of medieval monks. You’ve committed what the system sees as the greatest sin of all: you have lived *small*, without ever applying for the recognition to do so.”

Marnie’s eyes narrowed. “You’ve been watching us.”

“The ledger watches everyone. Smart meters, consumption profiles, social media sentiment analysis tied to geo-located purchase histories… it’s all just data points. CRLF simply has… better algorithms. A more nuanced parsing. We can see the poetry in the spreadsheets.” He tapped the leather suitcase. “And the poetry of your life, Ms. Vance, Mr. Bell, is a haiku of radical under-consumption. While your neighbours were installing patio heaters and subscribing to monthly mystery snack boxes from Thailand, you were learning to mend socks and brewing your own vinegar. You have, through a lifetime of quiet, conscious negation, accrued a significant carbon surplus.”

Arthur felt dizzy. It was the heat, surely. “A surplus. So we… owe less?”

“No, no. You *own* more. Carbon is a currency. The only real currency left. Governments deal in mandatory offsets, punitive taxes. That’s the crude, public economy. We operate in the private, speculative market. Your surplus—let’s call it 4.73 metric tonnes of verified, lifestyle-based carbon avoidance—is an asset. And a valuable one. There are entities… individuals, corporations, boutique nations… who need to balance their books in a more… *discreet* manner than the public exchanges allow.” He paused, letting the implication hang in the thick air. “They are willing to pay for it.”

Marnie uncrossed her arms. “Pay. With what? Money’s no good if the grid’s down and the water’s going.”

“Ah!” Finch’s smile widened. He unclasped the suitcase with a crisp *click*. Inside, nestled in grey foam, were not bundles of cash, but three objects.

The first was a cylinder, about the size of a thermos, brushed steel, with a single green LED light glowing on one end. The second was a flat, matte-black panel, flexible, like a placemat. The third was a small, ornate key, brass, with an intricate filigree bow.

“The currency of the new world,” Finch said softly. “Not store of value, but store of *function*.”

He lifted the cylinder. “This is a Hestia Core. A personal, low-emission thermal regulator. It doesn’t ‘cool’ the air in the crude, energy-gobbling way of old. It uses targeted phase-change materials and piezoelectric manipulation of localised air molecules to create a… comfort sphere. About a three-meter radius. Silent. Draws less power than a nightlight. Solar rechargeable.” He placed it on the floor and pressed the LED. A faint, almost imperceptible hum, like a cat’s purr, emanated from it. And then, like a miracle, like a forgotten dream, Arthur felt it. A gentle, cool dryness kissing his skin. The woolen thickness of the air began to thin, just around them. He gasped.

Marnie took an involuntary step into the sphere of influence. Her shoulders, which had been hunched near her ears for days, dropped. “My God.”

“The panel,” Finch continued, lifting the black sheet. “A Sun-Sip. High-efficiency photovoltaic, but with a twist. It works on diffuse light. Even this.” He gestured to the yellow haze outside the window. “It’s not about brilliant sunshine anymore. It’s about harvesting the gloom. This one panel can trickle-charge a bank of batteries to run lights, comms, a small fridge. Not a hope chest. A real fridge.”

He set it down. Finally, he picked up the brass key. “This is symbolic, but also quite literal. It is a key to a place called The Haven. A… community. In a bioclimatic zone that is currently, and is projected to remain, within the human habitability sweet spot. It is not a fortress. It is a series of restored limestone cottages with geothermal cooling, perennial polyculture gardens, and a library that contains actual paper books. Membership is by carbon equity contribution only. This key represents your share. Based on your surplus.”

The room was silent, save for the faint, blessed hum of the Hestia Core. The coolness was a physical shock, a balm. Arthur’s mind, sluggish with heat, tried to catch up. This was a scam. It had to be. A long-con tailored to the desperate and the sweaty.

“What’s the catch?” Marnie’s voice was hard, practical, cutting through the luxury of cool air. “We sign over our ‘surplus’ and you give us this… kit? And a key to a fairy tale?”

“The catch, Ms. Vance, is that the surplus is verified and *transferable*. Once transferred, it is gone from your ledger. You start at zero. The catch is that you must decide if you wish to cash in a lifetime of conscientious living for a measure of immediate comfort and a possible future. The catch is that the world outside is not getting better. The Brownout may be temporary, or it may be the first permanent dimming of the lights. The catch is…” he looked at them, and for the first time, his polished demeanour showed a hairline crack, a glimpse of something weary and real, “…that there is no morally pure path anymore. Only choices with different kinds of dirt on them.”

He reached into his suit jacket and produced two sheets of paper, creamy and thick. “The contract. One for each of you. It assigns your verified carbon surplus (the ‘Asset’) to the CRLF in exchange for the items specified (the ‘Consideration’). The Haven key is a separate deed. Read it. The language is quite clear.”

Arthur took his. The words swam. *Parties of the first part… hereby relinquish all claim and title… in perpetuity… consideration rendered in the form of one (1) Hestia Core Model 7…*

“How long do we have?” Marnie asked, her eyes scanning the page.

“The offer is firm for forty-eight hours. After that, the ledger… recalibrates. Market value fluctuates. And,” he added, standing up, “I have other appointments. The truly prepared are often the most reluctant to be saved. It’s a fascinating paradox.” He picked up his now-empty suitcase. “I’ll leave you with the Core running. A gesture of goodwill. I’ll return tomorrow evening for your decision.”

He tipped his hat again and let himself out, closing the front door softly behind him.

The hum of the Core filled the room. The cool air was an embrace. Arthur looked at Marnie. She was staring at the key, lying on the contract.

“It’s a bribe,” she said, finally. “A beautifully packaged, incredibly effective bribe.”

“Is it?” Arthur walked to the window. The snail was gone, having completed its epic journey across the glass. He looked out at the yellow street. Mr. Henderson was on his second coat of silver paint. The Carver twins were packing up their wagon. “What if it’s real? What if we *earned* this? Not in a ‘chosen one’ way, but in a… bean-counting, sock-mending, sigh-of-dread way?”

“Then it means the system found a way to commodify even that. Our guilt, our anxiety, our choice to not have kids, to not fly, to eat lentils until they come out of our ears—it’s all just been… *equity*. Waiting to be harvested.” She picked up the Sun-Sip panel. It was light, almost insubstantial. “This feels real. The cool air feels real.”

“So do the lentils,” Arthur said, a weak joke.

Marnie didn’t smile. She walked to the hope chest and lifted the lid. The cool air from the Core met the warmer, damp air from the ice melt. She looked at their slushy water bottles, their sweating cheese, their neighbour’s insulin. “We could have a fridge again, Arthur. A real one. We could have light after dark. We could stop fanning a box.”

“And in exchange, we let some… carbon hedge fund use our good behaviour to offset someone’s private jet? To let a petro-state prince feel carbon-neutral?”

“Probably.” Marnie closed the lid. “But the jet will fly anyway. The prince will buy someone else’s surplus. The carbon will be offset from a ledger somewhere. The only difference is whether we are cool and have light, or whether we are hot and in the dark, clinging to the moral high ground while our butter turns to oil.”

The simplicity of the calculus was horrifying. Arthur thought of the line at the community centre, the silent, desperate veneration of the dying freezer. That was the future: a collective, grinding poverty of comfort, a universal descent into the struggle for basics. Finch’s offer was a lifeboat. A gilded, morally ambiguous lifeboat, but a lifeboat nonetheless.

“The Haven,” Arthur said. “What do we even know about it?”

Marnie had already pulled her dead laptop from its shelf. It was useless. She threw it down in frustration. “We know nothing. We have to trust the man in the linen suit. That’s the heart of the scam, isn’t it? Create desperation, then offer a bespoke solution that requires total trust.”

They ate a silent dinner of crackers and the now-softening cheese, bathed in the miraculous cool hum. It was the most profound luxury Arthur had ever experienced. It made the rest of the house, just beyond the three-meter radius, feel like a hostile planet. It was a taste. A first, addictive hit.

As dusk fell, the yellow sky deepening to a bruised orange, they lit candles. The Core’s green LED was a tiny, steadfast star. They spread the contracts on the coffee table.

“We could try to verify it,” Arthur said, without conviction.

“How? Google is a memory. The library is closed. Our neighbours are painting their furniture silver and trading in bottlecaps.” Marnie traced the filigree on the brass key. “It’s a leap of faith. Or a fall into gullibility.”

A sound broke the evening stillness—a sharp, sustained *crack*. Then shouting from down the street. Arthur and Marnie went to the window. Two houses down, the Carver family was on their lawn, yelling. A large branch, desiccated by drought and heat, had sheared off the old maple in their front yard and crashed through their living room window. In the twilight, they could see the dark, jagged hole. The family’s voices were sharp with shock and despair. A small, everyday disaster. A window. But now, a window was a structural vulnerability, a breach in the fragile seal against the outside air. It would take weeks to fix. If it could be fixed at all.

Arthur looked from the scene of scrambling, distressed neighbours back to their own living room. The circle of cool, calm light. The contracts. The key.

Marnie was watching him. Her face was in shadow, but the candlelight caught the resolve in her eyes. “We’re not just buying comfort, Arthur,” she said, her voice low. “We’re buying *time*. Time to think. Time to not be in emergency mode every second of the day. Time to figure out what comes next.”

“And the cost?”

“We became carbon accountants the minute we started counting food miles,” she said, a bitter edge to her voice. “We just didn’t know we had a balance sheet. Now we do. Do we spend it, or do we let it evaporate in the heat?”

She picked up a pen from the coffee table. It hovered over the signature line on her contract.

The hum of the Core was the only sound. The cool air held them. Outside, the world was broken, hot, and dark. Inside, for three meters, was a fragile, purchased oasis.

Arthur picked up his own pen. The brass key glinted in the candlelight, promising limestone cottages and perennial gardens. A fairy tale. A lifeboat.

He looked at Marnie. A lifetime of shared sighs, of conscientious choices, of quiet, stubborn smallness. It had all been accruing. It was all on the line.

“For the butter,” he said, and the absurdity of it almost made him cry.

Marnie gave a single, sharp nod.

Their pens touched paper.

[Chapter 2 End - 2,512 words]

---

# Chapter 3

## CHAPTER 3

The hum stopped at precisely 10:17 the next morning. The green LED on the Hestia Core flickered, dimmed, and went dark. The coolness lingered for a few minutes, a ghost in the air, and then the heat rushed back in like a tide, reclaiming every inch of space. It felt heavier than before, more personal, as if it had been insulted by its brief banishment.

Arthur was sitting on the floor, staring at the dead cylinder. He’d been watching it for an hour, as if his attention could will it back to life. The contracts were signed. The key was in Marnie’s pocket, a cold, heavy secret. They had traded their invisible, ethical savings for three tangible things, and one of them had just died after less than a day.

“It needs sun,” Marnie said, her voice tight. She was on the porch, unrolling the Sun-Sip panel on the hot wooden planks. The jaundiced light fell on its matte surface. Nothing happened. No lights, no hum. “It’s supposed to work on diffuse light. This is the definition of diffuse.”

“Maybe it’s broken,” Arthur said. “Maybe it was always broken. A prop.”

“Don’t.” Marnie’s tone was a warning. She couldn’t entertain that thought, not after signing. She adjusted the panel’s angle, wiped a non-existent speck of dust. “It just needs a charge. A deep charge. We didn’t give it enough time yesterday.”

The day stretched before them, hot and interminable. The hope chest cooler was their reality again. The ice from their community centre quest was gone, fully transformed into tepid water they’d used to flush the toilet. The butter was officially oil. The cheese had developed a personality. Only Mrs. Gunderson’s insulin, suspended in its little vial, remained priority number one.

Finch was due back that evening. The plan—their fragile, desperate plan—had been to present a united front, to show him they were savvy beneficiaries, not desperate marks. They would have the Core humming, the panel charging a battery, and they would ask intelligent questions about The Haven’s water table and community governance. Now, they would be showing him two pieces of inert tech and a plea for a refund.

“We should try the key,” Arthur said, not moving from the floor.

“What?”

“The Haven. It’s supposed to be a place. There must be coordinates. An address. Something on the key or the deed.”

Marnie came inside, leaving the panel on the porch. She pulled the key from her pocket. They examined it under the weak light from the window. The filigree was intricate, but it was just decoration. There were no numbers, no hidden glyphs. The deed paper was the same creamy stock as the contract, with formal, legalese descriptions of a “shareholding interest in the Haven Mutual Assurance Collective” and “rights of habitation per the attached covenants (Exhibit A).” There was no Exhibit A.

“It’s a token,” Marnie said, her defiance crumbling. “A physical symbol for a digital entry. The real key is probably a biometric scan or a password. We traded 4.73 tonnes of our life for a paperweight, a placemat, and a souvenir.”

The silence that followed was the loudest yet. It was the sound of hope evaporating, faster than the last puddle of ice melt.

A knock at the door made them both jump. It wasn’t Finch’s polite tap. It was a firm, authoritative *rap-rap-rap*.

Arthur opened the door. It wasn’t Finch.

A woman stood there, maybe fifty, with a severe grey bob and wearing the khaki vest of the Neighborhood Resource Coordination Committee—a volunteer group that had sprung up after the Brownout. Her name tag read **GLENYS**. Beside her was a young man with a clipboard, sweating profusely in a polo shirt.

“Arthur Bell?” Glenys said, not waiting for confirmation. “We’re conducting a household resilience audit. Mandatory for the precinct. May we come in?”

It wasn’t really a question. She stepped inside, her eyes doing a swift, inventorying sweep. They lingered on the hope chest, the dead Core, the felting wool. The young man followed, already making notes.

“What’s this about?” Marnie asked, stepping forward.

“Resource pooling,” Glenys said brightly, but her eyes were flat. “The Brownout has revealed critical vulnerabilities. The Committee is mapping assets and deficits for community stability. We need to know what you have, and what you need.” She nodded to the young man. “Start with water.”

The young man, avoiding eye contact, asked, “How many litres of potable water do you currently have stored?”

“Enough,” Marnie said.

“We’ll need a specific figure for the ledger.”

Arthur felt a cold dread that had nothing to do with temperature. This was a different kind of ledger. “We have two five-gallon jugs. Mostly full.”

“Nine gallons. Approximately thirty-four litres.” The young man wrote it down. “Food? Non-perishable?”

“We’re fine,” Marnie said, her voice sharp.

“We have rice,” Arthur said, defeated. “Some canned beans.”

“Medical supplies? Prescription medications?”

“We have a neighbour’s insulin in our cooler,” Arthur said, then instantly regretted it.

Glenys’s head swivelled towards the hope chest. “Insulin? That requires refrigeration. Is your cooler functional?”

“It’s on ice,” Marnie snapped. “We’re managing.”

“Ice is a finite resource,” Glenys said, moving towards the chest. “Community centre slots are rationed. The Committee is centralizing temperature-sensitive medicines at the cooling hub at the old library. It’s generator-backed. For the common good.” She lifted the lid of the hope chest before Marnie could stop her. She saw the vial, nestled in the water. She saw the oily butter, the sweating cheese. Her nose wrinkled slightly. “This is not a viable solution. We’ll



---

# Chapter 4

## CHAPTER 4

The ledger, it turned out, was a living thing. It breathed in your rice, your beans, the cool blue vial of a neighbour’s insulin, and exhaled a carbon-copy receipt that felt lighter than a dead leaf. Glenys took the insulin. She took the butter, too, citing “lipid spoilage risk and associated olfactory offences in a confined community.” The cheese she left, after a long, considering look at its glistening rind. “We’ll log you for a generator-cooled storage slot,” she’d said, her voice the flat tone of a recorded announcement. “Priority tier four. Expect a summons.”

After they left, the silence in the flat was a physical presence, thick and hot as wool. Marnie stood staring at the closed door, her hands clenched into white-knuckled fists at her sides. Arthur watched the space where the insulin had been, a cold hollow in the pit of his stomach that no amount of sweat could warm.

“Tier four,” Marnie said, finally. The words were brittle. “We’re a tier-four problem.”

“We’re not a problem,” Arthur said, but it came out weak, a protest against the weather.

“We are now. We have a ledger entry.” She turned from the door, her movements stiff. She went to the hope chest and slammed the lid shut. The sound was like a bone breaking. “They know we have the felting wool. They noted it. ‘Craft supplies, non-essential.’ They know about the dead Core. ‘Defunct entertainment hardware.’ They’re making a list. Not of what we need. Of what we *are*.”

Arthur’s eyes drifted to the window. The sky had deepened from jaundiced yellow to a bruised purple-grey. The air felt charged, like the moment before a fuse catches. “What do we do?”

“We learn,” Marnie said. She looked at him, and the fury in her eyes had banked into something harder, sharper. “And we adapt. Fast.”

**Scene 1**

The trial began with water. Not the lack of it, but the profound, sloshing weight of it. The summons came not for a cooling slot, but for a water haul. A precinct-wide directive, delivered by a different young person with the same flat eyes: all able-bodied residents in tiers three through five were to report to the main standpipe at the old library for ration distribution and transport.

Arthur stood in a queue that coiled around the block like a lethargic serpent. The air was a soup of humidity and unwashed bodies, a collective exhalation of defeat. He held two empty five-gallon jugs, the plastic slick in his hands. Marnie was beside him, her own jugs at her feet. She was scanning the crowd, her gaze calculating.

“Don’t look eager,” she murmured. “But don’t look weak. Just look… capable of carrying things.”

“I *am* capable of carrying things,” Arthur whispered back, his shoulder already aching in anticipation.

“It’s not about the carrying. It’s about the not-dropping. The not-complaining. It’s an audition.”

For what, she didn’t say. But Arthur saw the evaluative glances from the people in orange vests—Committee auxiliaries—who moved along the line. They noted the man who swayed on his feet, the woman who argued about her place in the queue. They made small, tick-like marks on their clipboards.

The standpipe was a rusted iron mouth gushing a pathetic, brown-tinged stream. It took forty-five minutes to reach it. An auxiliary filled Arthur’s jugs, the water splashing his legs. The weight, when he lifted them, was astonishing. It was a weight that spoke of anchors, of drowning. He took a step, and the world tilted. The jugs were ungainly, pulling him in opposite directions. He was a poorly designed beast of burden.

The walk back was two kilometres. A pilgrimage of the tiered. Arthur fell into a rhythm: ten steps, shift grip, ten steps, swap sides. The handles bit into his palms. His back became a single, screaming knot of protest. He focused on the cracked pavement ahead of him, on the slow, shuffling steps of the man in front. He learned to lean into the weight, to let it become a part of his forward momentum, a terrible pendulum. He learned to ignore the warm trickle of what he hoped was sweat tracing his spine. He learned the specific, grinding agony of a task that was both mundane and essential.

Marnie, ahead of him, carried her jugs as if they were filled with feathers. Her stride was even, her posture erect. She was performing capability, and performing it flawlessly.

They passed a house where someone had set up a folding table. A sign, hand-lettered on cardboard, read: “COLD WATER – 1 CREDIT PER CUP.” Behind the table, a man with a face the colour of old clay sat beside a small, battery-powered cooler. The queue for his table was longer than the one for the standpipe.

Arthur saw an auxiliary stop, speak to the clay-faced man, and make a note. The man’s expression didn’t change. The ledger was breathing here, too.

Back at the flat, they decanted the water into every container they owned. The act felt sacred, and futile. Thirty-four litres logged, thirty-four litres hauled. A net gain of zero, but a profound loss of energy. Arthur’s hands were raw. His muscles trembled with a fine, constant vibration.

“Lesson one,” Marnie said, massishing her own shoulders. “They don’t give you anything. They just move the thing you already have from one place to another, and make you carry it. The movement is the point. The fatigue is the product.”

“What’s lesson two?” Arthur asked, slumped at the table.

“We need to not be tier four.”

**Scene 2**

The shared struggle was not against the heat, or the Brownout, but against the ledger’s relentless, categorizing breath. Their ally was Mrs. Ogden from flat five, though alliance was a strong word for the brittle, transactional partnerships forming in the building’s stale stairwell.

Mrs. Ogden had a solar charger. A small, folding panel that, when placed on the tiny, blistering-hot balcony of her flat, could coax enough trickle from the feverish sun to charge a phone battery in six hours. She also had a cat, a vast, persian creature named Tiberius who shed clouds of white fur and stared at the world with profound disappointment.

Marnie’s opening gambit was felting wool. She presented Mrs. Ogden with a small, tightly rolled bundle of russet-coloured roving. “For Tiberius,” she’d said. “A bed. It’s cooler than synthetic fibre.”

Mrs. Ogden, a woman built like a sparrow and with eyes just as bright and suspicious, had taken the wool. She’d felt its weight. “What do you want?”

“Information,” Marnie said. “And ten percent of a charge, twice a week.”

The information came first. Mrs. Ogden’s niece was an auxiliary. Low-level, but inside the system. The tiers, she whispered, were not just about need. They were about utility. Tier One were the engineers, the medics, the generator operators. Tier Two were the strong haulers, the organised queue-managers. Tier Three were the compliant, those with minor useful skills—sewing, basic repair. Tier Four were the drain. The elderly, the sick, the stubborn, the Arthur-and-Marnies of the world.

“And Tier Five?” Arthur asked.

Mrs. Ogden’s lips tightened. “Relocation.”

The word hung in the air, heavier than the water jugs.

“How do you move up?” Marnie’s voice was low, practical.

“You demonstrate asset surplus,” Mrs. Ogden said, parroting a phrase she’d clearly heard. “You have something the ledger wants to breathe in, that doesn’t leave you empty. Or you perform a consistent, quantifiable service. You become a cog that turns without squeaking.”

The shared struggle cemented that night. The building’s one functional tap, in the ground-floor laundry, began to spit air, then a rusty dribble, then nothing. A collective groan seemed to rise through the floorboards. Panic, quiet and sharp, filtered under doors.

Marnie went to Mrs. Ogden. Arthur followed. They found her staring at her own empty jugs, her small face pinched.

“We need a work group,” Marnie stated. “You have the information network. I have organisational capacity. Arthur has…” She looked at him. “Arthur has patience.”

“I do?” Arthur said.

“You watched a snail for seventeen minutes. That’s a kind of patience.”

They became a team of three. Mrs. Ogden used her solar charge to message her niece, learning that a pipe had burst two streets over, that the fix was a low priority. Marnie organised the building’s residents. She assigned a watch on the laundry room to prevent hoarding if the flow returned. She calculated the remaining water per flat, pooling a litre from each for a common emergency reserve. Arthur was sent to the roof, to check the building’s long-defunct water tank—a relic of a wetter age. He climbed the rusted ladder, pushed open the hatch, and found a cavern of dry, flaking metal and the skeletons of several pigeons. It was a cathedral of absence.

He reported back. Marnie didn’t flinch. “Okay. So we’re on haul detail for the foreseeable. We do it as a unit. We cover for each other. We share the load, literally.”

Mrs. Ogden, clutching a now-purring Tiberius (nestled happily in a felted wool prototype), nodded. “My niece says they notice cohesion. It’s a stability marker. It might… adjust our tier coefficient.”

Arthur felt it then, the bond. Not of affection, but of mutual leverage. They were three separate problems that, lashed together, might resemble a solution. Marnie was the strategist, the hard edge. Mrs. Ogden was the intelligence, the whisper in the dark. Arthur, he realised, was the weight-bearer. The one who could look at a hopeless, empty tank and simply note its dimensions. It was a role. It was definition.

**Scene 3**

The true nature of the opposing force was not Glenys, or the Committee, or even the Brownout. It was the system they had built—a system that saw people not as souls, but as columns of assets and deficits, their worth calculated in litres and labour units. Arthur discovered its clarity in the old library.

His summons for a generator-cooled storage slot finally came. The library was no longer a place for books. The shelves were pushed against the walls, stacked with plastic crates. The air hummed with the deep, diesel grumble of the generator, a sound so foreign and powerful it felt like a heartbeat from another world. The coolness was a shock. It was aggressive. It raised goosebumps on Arthur’s arms, a visceral memory of a forgotten state.

He was directed to a table where a man sat behind a laptop, its screen glowing with a crisp, blue light that seemed obscenely vibrant. The man wore a headset. He didn’t look up as Arthur approached.

“Name. Precinct. Tier.”

“Arthur Bell. Eight-Seven. Tier Four.”

Fingers tapped. “Purpose of requisition?”

“Temperature-sensitive storage. For… food.” He’d almost said ‘cheese’.

“Slot allocation is based on asset contribution to community stability. List any non-essential assets you have surrendered to the precinct ledger.”

Arthur thought of the insulin, the butter. “We surrendered medical supplies. And fats.”

“Logged.” More tapping. “List any ongoing stability services you perform.”

“Water haul. Building cohesion management.”

The man’s eyes flicked up, just for a second. He input something. “Cohesion metrics are unverified. Haul labour is baseline. Your slot allocation is twelve minutes, twice weekly. You may store one item, not exceeding two litres in volume or two kilograms in mass. You will be assigned a storage bin number. You are responsible for the secure transport of your asset to and from the hub. Any damage or loss is not the responsibility of the Committee. Do you understand?”

It was a liturgy. Arthur nodded.

“Verbal confirmation is required.”

“I understand.”

“Bin 174. Down the left aisle. A monitor will verify your item.”

Bin 174 was a small, clear plastic crate on a metal shelf. A young woman with an auxiliary badge stood nearby, holding a tablet. Arthur took the wax-paper wrapped parcel of cheese from his bag. The auxiliary scanned a barcode on the bin, then held out her hand for the cheese. She weighed it on a small scale, noted the weight on her tablet, then placed it in the bin. She scanned the bin again. A green light on the shelf blinked.

“Your time starts now,” she said, and walked away.

Arthur stood in the humming, cool vastness, and looked at his cheese in its clear plastic coffin. It was logged, weighed, tracked, and timered. Its preservation was a function of his tier, which was a function of his utility, which was being evaluated by his management of a building’s water panic and his ability to carry heavy things without dropping them. The cheese was no longer food. It was a data point in the ledger’s lung. The system was perfect. It was perfectly indifferent. It would preserve his cheese with mechanical efficiency while it decided whether or not he himself was worth the oxygen he breathed.

The enemy was not malice. It was the spreadsheet. It was the quiet, rational replacement of community with logistics.

**Scene 4**

The plan was Marnie’s. “We need an asset surplus,” she said, laying it out on their sticky kitchen table. “Something they need, that we can control the supply of. Not water. Not labour. Something they haven’t thought to ledger yet.”

Mrs. Ogden, sipping her ration of tepid water, said, “Information is an asset. My niece hears things. The generator at the library is unstable. The diesel supply is… creative. There are rumours of a deeper Brownout coming. A full Blackout. The Committee is terrified of it. Panic is their primary deficit.”

Arthur thought of the perfect, humming coolness. “What do they need to prevent panic?”

“Distraction,” Marnie said, her eyes landing on the dead Core console, a black rectangle of obsolescence. “Or comfort. A sense of normalcy.” Her gaze then shifted to the remaining felting wool, piled in a soft, colourful mound in the corner. “Or both.”

The idea was audacious. The library had a children’s section, now just a corner of crates. The Committee, according to Mrs. Ogden’s niece, wanted to run “stability workshops” there. To show life going on. Marnie proposed they offer a felting workshop. For children. “We provide the materials, the instruction. We create a visible, positive community activity. We become a source of cohesion, not just managers of it. That’s a high-value asset. That could bump us to Tier Three. Maybe even Two.”

It was a good plan. It leveraged Marnie’s skill, Mrs. Ogden’s connection, and Arthur’s patience. It was a small, woolly rebellion against the ledger.

The flaw was not in the plan, but in the planner. Marnie’s flaw was her contempt. She could perform compliance, but she could not hide her disdain for the system that demanded it. And the system had sensors for that.

They presented the proposal to Glenys, who had become their designated interface with the Committee. They did it in Mrs. Ogden’s flat, to show cohesion. Marnie was careful, polite. She framed it as an offering, a contribution.

Glenys listened, her flat eyes on Marnie’s face. She asked practical questions about material sourcing, duration, participant limits. Marnie answered smoothly. Arthur felt a flicker of hope. It was working.

Then Glenys said, “And who would lead this workshop?”

“I would,” Marnie said. “With support.”

“You have a background in education? Or child development?”

“I have a background in making things work,” Marnie said. The edge was there, a tiny, sharp sliver of the steel beneath.

Glenys’s gaze didn’t waver. “I see. And the felting wool. You have a significant supply. You listed it as ‘non-essential craft supplies.’ This would re-categorise it as a ‘community stability asset.’ That would be a favourable ledger adjustment.”

“Yes,” Marnie said, a hint of triumph in her voice.

“Where did you acquire such a quantity of wool?”

The question was a trapdoor. Arthur felt the bottom fall out of his stomach.

Marnie hesitated. A fraction of a second too long. “I… had it. From before.”

“From before,” Glenys echoed. “Inventory records from the precinct’s former arts cooperative are incomplete. There was a significant discrepancy in their stock. Several kilos of roving went unaccounted for. Before the Brownout.”

The silence was absolute. Tiberius stopped purring. Arthur could hear the faint, desperate buzz of a fly against Mrs. Ogden’s window.

“It was surplus,” Marnie said, her voice tight. “They were going to throw it out.”

“That is not the notation in the ledger,” Glenys said softly. She didn’t smile. She didn’t need to. “The ledger says: ‘unaccounted.’ It’s a deficit column. You propose to transform a deficit into a community asset. That is… interesting.”

She stood up. “The workshop proposal is noted. Pending review of asset provenance. Thank you for your contribution.”

After she left, the three of them sat in the suffocating quiet.

“She knows,” Mrs. Ogden whispered, her face ashen. “She knows you took it.”

“It was waste,” Marnie hissed, her fists clenched. “It was going in a skip!”

“It doesn’t matter,” Arthur said, the cold dread from the first audit returning, deeper now. “It’s in the ledger. You’re not a person who saved waste. You’re a deficit. A thief.”

The plan was jeopardized. Not by an external threat, but by a line in a ledger from a world that was gone. Marnie’s past action, her refusal to let a resource be wasted, had just been re-categorised. It was no longer initiative. It was a vulnerability. And Glenys, the agent of the ledger, had breathed it in. She would now decide what to exhale.

The hook for the next chapter was the look on Marnie’s face—not of fear, but of a cold, furious understanding. She had seen the true game. It wasn’t about survival. It was about ownership. The ledger sought to own everything, even the past. And it had just laid claim to hers.



---

# Chapter 5

## CHAPTER 5

The silence Glenys left behind had a texture. It was the thick, felted wool of a soundproofing panel, absorbing not just noise but hope. It sat in Mrs. Ogden’s front room for three full minutes, a third, suffocating guest. The fly gave up its buzzing. Tiberius, with a predator’s instinct for atmospheres gone wrong, had melted into the shadows beneath the sideboard.

Marnie was the first to move. She didn’t speak. She stood, walked to the kitchenette, and filled the kettle from the jerrycan. The *glug-glug-glug* of the water was obscenely loud. She placed the kettle on the hob with a precise *click*. Only then did she turn, leaning against the counter as if it were the only thing holding her up. Her face was not the furious mask Arthur had seen at the chapter’s hook. That had been the flash of steel. This was the cool metal after, the blade resting on the whetstone.

“A deficit,” she said. Her voice was flat. “I’m a line in a deficit column.”

“It’s bureaucratic sadism,” Mrs. Ogden said, wringing her hands in her lap. The wool of her cardigan sleeve was fraying. “They make the rules so you can’t help but break them, then punish you for the breaking. It’s how they maintain the… the…”

“The ledger,” Arthur finished. He was still on the sofa, feeling the ghost-impression of Glenys’s posture in the cushion. “It’s not a record. It’s an organism. It has to find things to eat. Deficits. Discrepancies. It just ate a piece of you.”

Marnie nodded, once. The kettle began its low, gathering rumble. “So. The workshop.”

“Is impossible,” Mrs. Ogden whispered.

“Is contingent,” Marnie corrected. She pushed off the counter. “On the provenance of the asset. She didn’t say no. She said ‘pending.’ She’s left it dangling. A carrot made of my own past.”

Arthur saw it then, the calculation behind her eyes. It was the same look she’d had when assessing the structural integrity of a collapsing carport, or the viability of a rusted bicycle chain. The problem had been redefined. It was no longer about wool. It was about the ledger’s hunger, and how to feed it without being the meal.

“What does she want?” Arthur asked.

“Compliance,” Mrs. Ogden said.

“Leverage,” Marnie said at the same time.

The kettle screamed. Marnie made tea with a ceremonial slowness. Three chipped mugs, a precious half-teaspoon of black tea leaves reused for the third time, the hot water poured with a steady hand. She brought them over, setting them down on the cork coasters with finality.

“She’s an auditor,” Marnie said, sitting. “Her currency isn’t goods. It’s transgression. She found one. Now she owns it. She’ll hold it until she decides what it’s worth to her. Or until she finds a bigger one.”

“So we find a bigger one to give her,” Arthur said. The idea formed like a bruise, ugly and inevitable.

Marnie sipped her tea, her gaze fixed on the dark liquid. “We don’t have one. We’re very small fish, Arthur. Our transgressions are the pathetic kind. Hoarding an extra can of beans. Using rainwater for a shower without logging it. Petty deficit. She wants capital.”

The room felt smaller. The yellow light through Mrs. Ogden’s net curtains seemed to press in, heavy and stale. Arthur thought of the snail on his window. Existential tenacity. That’s what they had. But tenacity against what? Against the slow, desiccating heat, yes. Against scarcity, maybe. But against a system that could reach back into a dead world and pluck out a forgotten line in a forgotten book, and use it to veto the future? His tenacity felt suddenly mucous-thin.

“Then the workshop is dead,” he said.

“No,” Marnie said, and the word was a nail hammered into the floor. “The workshop is the only thing that isn’t dead. It’s the one thing we’ve made that’s *new*. Not salvage. Not ration. A new skill. A new… product. Glenys sees that. That’s what’s interesting to her. Not the wool. The fact that we made something from the wool that the ledger doesn’t have a category for yet. She can’t control it. So she has to own the source.”

She put her mug down. “We give her the workshop.”

Mrs. Ogden gasped. “After all this?”

“Not the thing itself. The *credit*. We let her put her name on it. We make it her initiative. ‘Community cohesion through resource re-education.’ She gets to write a positive entry. A surplus of goodwill. It balances my deficit.”

Arthur felt a cold admiration for the move. It was a sacrifice, but a strategic one. Like leaving a trail of crumbs for a predator so it wouldn’t notice you were also carrying meat.

“She’ll accept that?” he asked.

“She’ll *consider* it,” Marnie said. “It turns a problem into an opportunity for her. Auditors are judged on net outcomes. Turning a thief into a community leader is a better story than just punishing a thief.”

“But you’re not a thief,” Mrs. Ogden said, her voice quavering.

Marnie looked at her, and for a moment, the cold mask slipped. There was a vast, weary sadness there. “Mrs. O, in the world of the ledger, I am exactly what it says I am. Reality is the footnote.”

The plan, then, was not to fight the ledger, but to edit it. A collaborative revision. The thought made Arthur’s skin crawl, but the alternative was a hard, flat ‘no’ from a woman who held the keys to every communal lock in the precinct. They spent the next hour drafting the language of their surrender. Marnie would write a formal note to Glenys, expressing her regret for the “administrative oversight” regarding the wool’s provenance, and her enthusiastic desire to place the nascent workshop under the auspices of the Precinct Resource Office for “proper development and oversight.” They would frame it as their own humble suggestion, born of a desire for procedural correctness.

It was a lie, of course. A sleek, polished lie. It felt like swallowing a stone.

***

The journey to the Resource Office was not long in distance—three streets over, in the old municipal library—but it was a voyage through a new kind of terrain. The “major challenge” was not a dragon or a dark lord; it was the act of walking their capitulation to the dragon’s lair. They went as a delegation of two: Marnie, the penitent, and Arthur, the moral support. Mrs. Ogden stayed behind, too agitated to be of use, promising to “hold the fort,” though the fort was just her front room and a cat with a dubious sense of loyalty.

The air outside was the usual broth. The jaundiced sky had deepened to a sickly ochre. Nothing grew in the small, hard-packed front gardens they passed. Life had retreated into cracks and corners. A lizard, desiccated and still as a fallen leaf, lay on a path. It was impossible to tell if it was alive or dead. The line between the two had blurred in the heat.

Arthur carried the note, folded neatly in an envelope they had salvaged from a water-damaged box in Mrs. Ogden’s attic. The flap didn’t stick properly. It felt like a metaphor.

“Remember,” Marnie said, her eyes on the pavement ahead. “We’re grateful for the guidance. We see the value of structure.”

“We’re happy to be absorbed,” Arthur muttered.

“Don’t mumble. And don’t use words like ‘absorbed.’ Use ‘integrated.’ Integration is positive. Absorption is what sponges do.”

They turned onto the high street. The library was a low, brutalist block of grey concrete, now streaked with rust-colored water stains from its long-dead air conditioning units. The glass doors were gone, replaced by heavy, salvaged timber doors with a small, grilled window. A hand-painted sign read: PRECINCT 7 RESOURCE ADMINISTRATION. SUBMISSIONS 9-11AM, 2-4PM.

It was 2:07.

Marnie took a breath that was not quite a sigh, and pushed the door.

The interior was a monument to the ledger made flesh. The grand, open space of the library had been partitioned into a maze of plywood cubicles. The smell was profound: dust, mildew, hot paper, and the faint, sweet-rotten scent of aging adhesive. The great books were gone, presumably pulped for fuel or insulation. In their place were boxes. Cardboard boxes, plastic crates, wooden crates, stacked in precarious towers, each labeled with a string of alphanumeric codes. The only light came from the high, narrow windows, cutting shafts through the dusty air where motes danced like static.

A young man with a pallor that suggested he hadn’t left the building in months sat at a makeshift desk just inside the door. He had a clipboard. He did not look up.

“Submission?” he said, to the clipboard.

“For Glenys,” Marnie said. “Regarding the community workshop proposal.”

“Glenys doesn’t do direct submissions before third review,” the young man recited. “Box B-14. Use the provided forms. Black ink only.”

Arthur felt a spike of irrational anger. This was the journey to the villain’s castle, and the drawbridge was manned by a bored goblin with a procedural handbook.

“This is a supplementary note,” Marnie said, her voice straining to remain pleasant. “Regarding asset provenance. It’s time-sensitive to her earlier review.”

The young man’s pen paused. He looked up, his eyes magnified by thick glasses. He assessed them, his gaze lingering on the dust on their shoes, the mended tear on Arthur’s shirt collar. He was categorizing them. Filing them in a mental B-box.

“Wait,” he said.

He stood and disappeared into the canyon of boxes. They heard his footsteps recede, then a low murmur. He returned, followed not by Glenys, but by a woman Arthur recognized: Anya, the quiet, sharp-eyed woman from the first audit, Glenys’s shadow. She held a tablet, its screen dark.

“Marnie. Arthur,” Anya said. Her voice was neutral, efficient. “Glenys is in reconciliation. You have something?”

Marnie held out the envelope. “A clarification. And a proposal for a streamlined administrative path for the workshop.”

Anya took the envelope, her fingers brushing Marnie’s. She didn’t open it. She simply held it. “The deficit has been logged. Clarifications are noted as amendments to the original entry. Was there new, physical evidence to submit?”

“No, it’s just—“

“Then this constitutes an amendment. I’ll add it to the file.” She turned to go.

“Will she see it?” Arthur asked, the words out before he could stop them.

Anya paused, half-turning back. Her expression was one of mild, professional pity. “Everything is seen, Arthur. The question is the priority of the seeing. Your file is active. It will be seen.” She offered a thin, non-smile. “You’ll receive a summons if a response is required.”

And with that, she was gone, swallowed by the maze.

The young man sat back down, picked up his pen. The audience was over.

Back on the street, the heat felt like a physical blow after the damp gloom of the library.

“Well,” Marnie said, staring at the closed door. “We’ve been entered into the system.”

“We’re an active file,” Arthur said, the phrase tasting of metal.

“We’re in the queue,” she corrected, starting the walk back. But her shoulders were set, her stride too brisk. The journey had been a failure. They had not parleyed with the dragon. They had fed their missive to a lesser worm in its gut. The central ordeal, Arthur realized, was not a meeting. It was the wait.

***

The final preparations were not physical. They were psychological. A siege mentality, but the army outside was invisible, its weapons were notations, and its battle cry was silence.

They gathered again in Mrs. Ogden’s front room that evening. The atmosphere had changed. Before Glenys’s visit, there had been a nervous energy, the thrill of a shared secret project. Now, there was only a grim vigilance. They were waiting for the ledger to speak.

Mrs. Ogden had attempted to bake a kind of biscuit from ground oats and a precious smear of honey. They were hard, dry things, but they ate them anyway, for the ritual of it. Tiberius watched from the top of the sideboard, his tail twitching.

“What’s the worst she can do?” Mrs. Ogden asked, crumbling a biscuit on her saucer.

“Requisition the wool,” Arthur said. “Formally charge Marnie with misappropriation. Penalty points. Reduced rations. Community service detail cleaning the solar panel arrays.”

“They wouldn’t,” Mrs. Ogden said, but it was a plea, not a statement.

“They would,” Marnie said quietly. She was spinning. She had brought a handful of the contraband wool and her drop spindle, and her hands moved in a steady, hypnotic rhythm, drafting the fibers into a thin, consistent thread. It was the only calm thing in the room. “The penalty isn’t the point. The point is the entry. Once it’s in the ledger as a transgression punished, it becomes a fact. It becomes *her*. Marnie, the one who stole. Everything else she does is filtered through that. Forever.”

Arthur watched her hands. The twist traveling up the thread, locking the fibers together. That’s what the ledger did. It took disparate events—a decision made in desperation years ago, a hopeful idea for a workshop—and twisted them together into a single, defining narrative. A story it could file.

“What if we just did it?” he said. The words surprised him. “What if we just held the workshop? In secret. In someone’s basement. Forget the approval.”

Marnie’s spindle paused for a single revolution, then continued. “And then what? Someone gets a skein of lovely grey yarn. They’re proud. They show a friend. The friend mentions it to a ration clerk. The clerk notes a discrepancy—where did you get the wool?—and reports it to earn credit. The audit arrives. The workshop is shut down. The wool is seized. The participants are penalized for receiving unlogged goods. The ledger wins bigger. It gets more to eat.” She shook her head. “You can’t build a free thing inside a cage. The cage just gets a new fixture.”

The truth of it settled over them. The freedom they had imagined—the freedom to create, to share a skill—was an illusion. It was a permission, and permissions could be revoked. The real ordeal was understanding the boundaries of the cage.

Arthur’s doubt was not about their plan. It was about the very premise of a plan. He felt a profound internal conflict, not between courage and fear, but between two kinds of resignation. One said: work within the system, edit the story, survive. The other, a colder, quieter voice, whispered: any story you write in their book is theirs.

He voiced none of this. He ate his dry biscuit and watched Marnie spin, the thread growing longer, stronger, and infinitely more fragile.

***

The summons came two days later. It was not delivered by Anya or the pale goblin. It was delivered by a child, a girl of maybe eight, who knocked on Mrs. Ogden’s door and held out a slip of recycled paper with a string of numbers and a time: 4:00 PM.

“For the workshop lady,” the girl said, and scampered off before anyone could ask a question.

The paper was the threshold. Holding it, Arthur felt the same hollow dread as when he’d seen the snail on his window, a harbinger of a different, smaller end. They were to cross into the dangerous zone, the administrative heart where the central ordeal would take place.

They arrived at 3:58. The timber door was propped open with a brick, letting a slab of hot, dead light fall into the library foyer. The goblin was gone. The cubicle maze was silent. The only sound was the faint, rhythmic tap of a single keyboard coming from deep within the stacks.

“Hello?” Marnie called. Her voice was swallowed by the boxes.

The tapping stopped.

Glenys emerged from an aisle to their right. She wore a different linen suit, same cut, a pale grey. She held a tablet, its glow casting her face in a cool, upward light. She did not smile.

“Marnie. Arthur. Follow me.”

She led them not to a desk or an office, but deeper into the maze. They passed towering columns of boxed files, pallets of blank forms, shelves of ink cartridges guarded like gold. They arrived at a small clearing in the center of the room. Here, a single, ancient library table remained. On it sat a large, leather-bound ledger, open. Not a tablet. An actual book. Its pages were thick, cream-colored, filled with handwritten entries in neat, black ink. Next to it lay their envelope, unopened.

Glenys gestured to two wooden chairs facing the table. She took the seat on the other side, placing her tablet beside the great book. She laced her fingers together.

“The workshop proposal,” she began, “has been reviewed in the context of the full asset history.”

Arthur’s heart was a trapped bird in his ribs. Marnie sat perfectly still.

“The deficit from the former arts cooperative is a matter of record. It is a closed file. However, the reappearance of the asset… reopens it.” Glenys’s eyes were on Marnie. “Your note suggested a path of administrative integration. A commendable impulse.”

She paused, letting the word ‘commendable’ hang in the dusty air. It was not a compliment; it was an assessment.

“However,” she continued, “integration requires a stable foundation. A deficit is instability. It must be resolved before new structures can be built upon it.”

“How?” Marnie asked, her voice barely a whisper.

Glenys opened the ledger. She turned pages, heavy and slow, until she found the entry. Arthur could see the dense script from across the table. Glenys ran a finger down a column. “The deficit is quantified. 4.2 kilograms of merino roving, unaccounted for. To resolve it, the asset must be returned, or its equivalent value must be added to the communal balance.”

“I don’t have 4.2 kilos of wool,” Marnie said. “I’ve used some. For samples.”

“Then equivalent value.” Glenys closed the ledger. She picked up her tablet, woke the screen, and turned it to face them. It displayed a simple list. *Precinct 7 – Outstanding Community Contribution Deficits.*
There were names. Next to each name, a task. *R. Alvarez: 18 hrs solar array scrubbing. L. Chen: 40 hrs water pipe insulation lagging.* And so on.

Glenys’s finger scrolled to the bottom. A new line had been added.
*M. Pembrooke: 4.2kg asset deficit. Resolution: Precinct-wide skills audit and cataloguing. 60 hrs.*

Arthur stared. A skills audit. Not cleaning panels. Not lagging pipes.

“What does that mean?” Marnie asked.

“The ledger records objects. Resources. It is poor at recording intangible assets. Skills. Knowledge. These are community resources that remain… unlogged. A deficit of a different kind.” Glenys’s gaze was unwavering. “You will visit every household in Precinct 7. You will inventory every non-essential skill its members possess. Carpentry. Knitting. Storytelling. Plant identification. Basic mechanics. You will log it. Categorize it. Assess its utility level. You will create a new ledger. A skills ledger.”

The air left Arthur’s lungs. It was a masterstroke. It was not punishment. It was co-option. It was turning Marnie, the would-be teacher, into the agent of the very system that sought to own her. She would become the census-taker of creativity, the taxman of talent. The workshop would be allowed to proceed—a footnote to this larger project, a demonstration of the system’s benevolence. But its founder would be forever transformed. She would no longer be a person with a skill to share. She would be the administrator of the skill-sharing file.

Marnie was silent for a long time. She looked at the tablet, then at the great leather-bound ledger, then at her own hands, resting on her knees. Arthur saw the understanding dawn, colder and more furious than before. This was the true game. It wasn’t just about owning the past. It was about owning the future, the very potential inside people’s heads. The ledger sought to own imagination itself.

“And if I refuse?” Marnie said, her voice dangerously flat.

Glenys leaned back. “Then the deficit stands. The wool is reclaimed. The workshop is cancelled. And your ration category is re-evaluated. Transgressors cannot be trusted with community-building initiatives. It would be a negative entry.” She tilted her head. “But you’re not a transgressor, are you, Marnie? You’re someone who wants to contribute. To integrate. This is integration. You help us see what we have. We help you… absolve your past.”

The hook was not a cliffhanger of action. It was the slow, silent shattering of a premise. The ordeal was not a battle they could fight. It was a choice between two kinds of servitude: to be crushed by the ledger, or to become its scribe.

Marnie looked at Arthur. Her eyes held no plea, no question. They were simply showing him the landscape of their new world. Then she turned back to Glenys.

“I’ll do it,” she said.

Glenys gave a single, slow nod. She picked up a pen from the table—a proper fountain pen—and opened the great ledger. She wrote, in her flawless script, next to Marnie’s name: *Resolution in progress.*

“Start with the eastern quadrant,” she said, not looking up. “Anya will provide you with the forms. Black ink only.”

They were dismissed. As they walked back through the canyon of boxes, towards the yellow light of the failing day, Arthur felt the threshold they had crossed. It was not made of stone or guarded by beasts. It was made of lines on paper, and they had just stepped inside. The dangerous zone was not a place. It was a role. Marnie had become a function of the ledger. And he, by association, was now part of its grammar.

The world had not ended with the air conditioning. It was ending like this: with a quiet assent, in a room full of boxes, as a pen scratched a person’s soul into a column of a book that never closed.



---

# Chapter 6

## CHAPTER 6

The walk back from the Administration Block was not measured in steps, but in the thickness of the new silence that had congealed between them. It was a physical substance, this silence, viscous and grey as the snail’s trail. Arthur felt it clinging to his skin, sealing his mouth. Marnie moved beside him, a study in contained velocity, her eyes fixed on the cracked pavement ahead as if reading a map of faults. The jaundiced sky had deepened to a sickly ochre, the sun a blurred coin behind a sheet of soiled muslin.

They did not speak of the ledger. To speak of it would be to grant its reality a dimension outside of that room, to let its ink bleed into the open air. Arthur understood, with a dull, hydraulic certainty, that this was the true function of the boxes in Glenys’s office. They were not for storage. They were baffles, sound-deadening walls to contain the specific frequency of a soul being notarized. The transaction had happened in a vacuum. Now they carried the vacuum with them.

The dangerous zone was a role. Marnie had become a function of the ledger.

They reached the workshop, the old gymnasium with its high, cobwebbed windows and permanent smell of damp concrete and machine oil. The familiar chaos of looms, carders, and half-finished projects usually felt like a defiance, a nest built in the eaves of the new world. Now it looked like evidence. Every spindle of wool, every dyebath, every sketched pattern on the wall was a line item, waiting to be tallied. Anya was there, her back to them, sorting a basket of dark fleece. She turned, and her smile—a habitual, weary crease—froze before it could fully form. She saw the vacuum in their faces.

“Forms,” Marnie said, the word a flat stone dropped on the workbench. “She said you’d have them.”

Anya’s hands, flecked with lanolin, went still. She didn’t ask who ‘she’ was. She only nodded, once, and moved to a metal filing cabinet in the corner, its drawers screeching a protest that echoed in the hollow gym. She pulled out a sheaf of papers clipped to a board, her movements precise and funeral. She handed it to Marnie without meeting her eyes.

The top sheet was a grid. Columns for Name, Residence Block, Ration Category, Observed Skill/Resource, Estimated Community Value, Notes on Disposition. At the top, in typed letters: *Eastern Quadrant – Initial Survey. Black ink only.*

Marnie took the board. Her fingers left no mark on the pristine paper. “I’ll start tomorrow.”

“Marnie,” Anya began, then stopped. Her gaze flicked to Arthur, then to the high windows, as if looking for a witness that wasn’t there. “The wool… for the winter project. Glenys approved the release. It’s here.” She gestured to a sack in the corner. “It’s yours. To distribute. As you see fit.”

It was not a gift. It was a first payment. The wool, once a symbol of communal effort, was now a retainer. Arthur watched Marnie absorb this. She didn’t look at the sack. She looked at the form, at the empty lines waiting to be filled with the fragile economies of their neighbors’ lives—who had a hidden knack for mending boots, who hoarded seeds, who could tell stories that kept children quiet during the long, dark curfews.

“Right,” Marnie said. She tucked the board under her arm. “Come on, Arthur.”

He followed her out, the sack of wool a heavy, accusing ghost between them. They didn’t go to their assigned bunkroom in D-block. Instead, Marnie led him to the old boiler room, a subterranean space behind the gym that was officially off-limits due to “structural concerns.” Their place. It was here, months ago, they’d found a rusted kettle and two chipped mugs, and had declared it a sanctuary. A space with no category.

Marnie lit the single fat candle with a wooden match. The flame guttered, then steadied, painting the cavern of pipes and brick in shuddering orange. She placed the survey board on an upturned crate, carefully, as if it were a live mine. Then she sat on the floor, her back against the warm, dormant boiler, and put her face in her hands.

Arthur stood by the door, holding the sack. He waited. The silence here was different; it was their own, a familiar, worn fabric. He could almost see the thoughts moving behind her hands, like creatures under ice.

“It’s not spying,” she said finally, her voice muffled. She lifted her head. The candlelight caught the tracks of sweat, or something else, on her temples. “That’s what she wants us to think. That’s the hook. The shame of it. To make us feel like traitors so we commit to it fully, to prove we’re not.”

Arthur let the sack slump to the floor. “What is it, then?”

“It’s inventory.” Marnie’s eyes were on the flame, black and depthless. “You don’t feel guilty taking inventory. You’re just counting. Assessing yield. It’s administrative. It’s clean.” She spoke the words as if tasting them, testing their bitterness. “She’s not asking me to betray anyone. She’s asking me to *appreciate* them. To see their worth, quantify it, and hand it over. She’s making me her appraiser.”

Arthur understood. The genius of it was surgical. The ledger didn’t want snitches. It wanted converts. It wanted you to look at your world through its lens until your vision permanently altered, until a person’s kindness was a “social lubricant asset” and a child’s curiosity was “untapped developmental potential.” You didn’t fight the ledger. You learned its language, and in doing so, forgot your own.

“So we don’t do it,” he said, the statement hanging in the hot, still air, naive as a soap bubble.

Marnie gave a short, dry laugh that held no humor. “The wool is reclaimed. The workshop is cancelled. Our rations are cut. We become a negative entry. A deficit.” She leaned forward, the shadows hollowing her cheeks. “Arthur, a deficit doesn’t just *suffer*. It serves as a lesson. It’s put on display. It’s given the worst jobs, the most public degradations, until it either corrects or… ceases to be a meaningful data point.”

He saw it. Not a beating in an alley. A slow, meticulous unraveling. Being moved to a pallet by the latrines. Your name called last for watery stew. The gradual avoidance by others, because association with a deficit carries a tax. You wouldn’t be killed. You’d be edited out.

“Then what?” His voice was a whisper.

“Then we do it,” she said. “But we do it wrong.”

---

**Scene 1: The protagonist faces their greatest fear or a seemingly insurmountable obstacle in a high-stakes confrontation.**

The wrongness began subtly.

Arthur started with the eastern quadrant’s water collection detail the next morning. His job, as Marnie’s “assistant,” was to observe and provide “context.” He stood at the edge of the old playground where the rain barrels were lined up, clipboard in hand, feeling the sun bake the part in his hair. The fear wasn’t of Glenys, or of getting caught. It was a deeper, more existential dread: the fear of his own gaze becoming instrumental.
